{"id":350924,"title":"Pink Floyd, 'The Wall'","description":"","body":"<p><em>Columbia, 1979</em></p><p>Pink Floyd&apos;s most elaborately theatrical album was inspired by their own success: the alienating enormity of their tours after <em>The Dark Side of the Moon </em>[see No. 43]. {"id":350955,"title":"Prince and the Revolution, 'Purple Rain'","description":"","body":"<p><em>Warner Bros., 1984</em></p><p>The blockbuster soundtrack to Prince&apos;s semiautobiographical movie was raunchy enough to inspire the formation of the censorship watchdog group Parents&apos; Music Resource Center. It also show&#xAD;cased Prince&apos;s abilities as a guitarist, especially on &quot;Let&apos;s Go Crazy.&quot; But at heart, <em>Purple Rain</em> is defined by its brilliant idiosyncrasies. {"id":351005,"title":"James Brown, 'Live at the Apollo'","description":"","body":"<p><em>King, 1963</em></p><p>Perhaps the greatest live album ever recorded. {"id":351016,"title":"The Beatles, 'Abbey Road'","description":"","body":"<p><em>Capitol, 1969</em></p><p>&quot;It was a very happy record,&quot; said producer George Martin. &quot;I guess it was happy because everybody thought it was going to be the last.&quot; <em>Abbey Road</em> &#x2013; recorded mostly in two months during the summer of 1969 &#x2013; almost never got made at all. {"id":351019,"title":"The Velvet Underground and Nico, 'The Velvet Underground'","description":"","body":"<p><em>Verve, 1967</em></p><p>&quot;We were trying to do a Phil Spector thing with as few instruments as possible,&quot; John Cale, the classically trained pianist and viola player of the Velvet Underground, said of this record. {"id":351018,"title":"Miles Davis, 'Kind of Blue'","description":"","body":"<p><em>Columbia, 1959</em></p><p>This painterly masterpiece would become one of the most important, influential and popular albums in jazz. But at the time it was made, <em>Kind of Blue</em> was a revolution all its own, a radical break from everything going on. Turning his back on standard chord progressions, trumpeter Miles Davis used modal scales as a starting point for composition and improvisation &#x2013; breaking new ground with warmth, subtlety and understatement in the thick of hard bop. Davis and his peerless band &#x2013; bassist Paul Chambers, drummer Jimmy Cobb, pianist Bill Evans, and the titanic sax team of John Coltrane and Cannonball Adderley &#x2013; soloed in uncluttered settings, typified by &quot;melodic rather than harmonic variation,&quot; as Davis put it. Two numbers, &quot;All Blues&quot; and &quot;Freddie Freeloader&quot; (the latter featured Wynton Kelly at the ivories in place of Evans), were in 12-bar form, but Davis&apos; approach allowed his players a cool, new, collected freedom. Evans wrote in his original liner notes, &quot;Miles conceived these settings only hours before the re&#xAD;cording dates and arrived with sketches which indicated to the group what was to be played. Therefore, you will hear something close to pure spontaneity in these performances.&quot; Or as the late critic Robert Palmer wrote, &quot;<em>Kind of Blue</em> is, in a sense, all melody &#x2013; and atmosphere.&quot; The bass line in &quot;So What&quot; is now among the most familiar obbligatos in jazz, and there is no finer evocation of the late-night wonder of jazz than the muted horns in &quot;All Blues.&quot;</p>","slug":"/music/lists/500-greatest-albums-of-all-time-20120531/miles-davis-kind-of-blue-20120524","order":490,"publish_date":"2012-05-24T16:18:11.000Z","updated_publish_date":null,"author":null,"media":{"lead":{"id":"","alt":"","code":"","type":"Variable Height Image","credits":"","details":"","filename":"","provider":""},"main_image":{"alt":null,"align":"full","caption":null,"credits":null,"filename":"rs-136801-7d7508478e8e401003325d5d0aaac731560d13ab.jpg","leadType":"Variable Height Image"}},"bitly":"","embeds":{"related":{"content":{"pinned":[]}}},"sequence":12}
{"id":351017,"title":"Elvis Presley, 'The Sun Sessions'","description":"","body":"<p><em>RCA, 1999</em></p><p>Many believe rock &amp; roll was born on July 5th, 1954, at Sun Studio in Memphis. Elvis Presley, guitarist Scotty Moore and bassist Bill Black were horsing around with &quot;That&apos;s All Right, Mama,&quot; a tune by bluesman Arthur &quot;Big Boy&quot; Crudup, when producer Sam Phillips stopped them and asked, &quot;What are you doing?&quot; &quot;We don&apos;t know,&quot; they said. Phillips told them to &quot;back up and do it again.&quot; The A side of Presley&apos;s first single (backed with a version of Bill Monroe&apos;s &quot;Blue Moon of Kentucky&quot;), &quot;That&apos;s All Right&quot; was issued by Sun on July 19th. It may or may not be the first rock &amp; roll record. But the man who would be King was officially on wax. Bridging black and white, country and blues, his sound was playful and revolutionary, charged by a spontaneity and freedom that changed the world. &quot;It&apos;s the blues,&quot; critic Greil Marcus wrote in his classic book <em>Mystery Train</em>. &quot;But free of all worry, all sin; a simple joy with no price to pay.&quot; Presley released four more singles on Sun &#x2013; including definitive reinventions of Wynonie Harris&apos; &quot;Good Rockin&apos; Tonight&quot; and Junior Parker&apos;s &quot;Mystery Train&quot; &#x2013; before moving on to immortality when Phillips sold his contract to RCA for $35,000. Incredibly, it took more than 20 years for Presley&apos;s Sun output to be properly collected on a 1976 LP &#x2013; which has since been superseded by this 1999 double-CD chronicle of the King&apos;s beginnings at Sun. It collects everything he cut at the studio, including alternate takes and the 1953 acetate he recorded as a gift for his mother as a shy and awkward recent high school graduate.</p>","slug":"/music/lists/500-greatest-albums-of-all-time-20120531/elvis-presley-the-sun-sessions-20120530","order":491,"publish_date":"2012-05-30T18:48:35.000Z","updated_publish_date":null,"author":null,"media":{"lead":{"id":"","alt":"","code":"","type":"Variable Height Image","credits":"","details":"","filename":"","provider":""},"main_image":{"alt":null,"align":"full","caption":null,"credits":null,"filename":"rs-136800-7a33165e92d530dd0f7c190f9dd46f81b95c5913.jpg","leadType":"Variable Height Image"}},"bitly":"","embeds":{"related":{"content":{"pinned":[]}}},"sequence":11}
{"id":351020,"title":"The Beatles, 'The White Album'","description":"","body":"<p><em>Capitol, 1968</em></p><p>They wrote the songs while on retreat with the Maharishi Mahesh Yogi in India, taking a break from the celebrity whirl. As John Lennon later said, &quot;We sat in the mountains eating lousy vegetarian food and writing all these songs.&quot; They came back with more great tunes than they could fit on a single LP, and competed fiercely during the sessions. &quot;I remember having three studios operating at the same time,&quot; George Harrison recalled. &quot;Paul was doing some overdubs in one, John was in another, and I was recording some horns or something in a third.&quot; The sessions became so tense that Ringo Starr quit the band in frustration for two weeks. Yet the creative tension resulted in one of the most intense and adventurous rock albums ever made. Lennon pursued his hard-edged vision into the cynical wit of &quot;Sexy Sadie&quot; and &quot;Happiness Is a Warm Gun,&quot; but also infused &quot;Julia&quot; and &quot;Dear Prudence&quot; with childlike yearning. {"id":351024,"title":"Bob Dylan, 'Blonde on Blonde'","description":"","body":"<p><em>Columbia, 1966</em></p><p>Released on May 16th, 1966, rock&apos;s first studio double LP by a major artist was, as Dylan declared in 1978, &quot;the closest I ever got to the sound I hear in my mind... that thin, that wild-mercury sound.&quot; There is no better description of the album&apos;s manic brilliance. After several false-start sessions in New York in the fall of 1965 and January 1966 with his killer road band the Hawks &#x2013; &quot;One of Us Must Know (Sooner or Later)&quot; was the only keeper &#x2013; Dylan blazed through the rest of Blonde on Blonde&apos;s 14 tracks in one four-day run and one three-day run at Columbia&apos;s Nashville studios in February and March 1966.</p><p>The pace of recording echoed the amphetamine velocity of Dylan&apos;s songwriting and touring schedule at the time. {"id":351021,"title":"The Clash, 'London Calling'","description":"","body":"<p><em>Epic, 1980</em></p><p>Recorded in 1979 in London, which was then wrenched by surging unemployment and drug addiction, and released in America in January 1980, the dawn of an uncertain decade, <em>London Calling</em> is 19 songs of apocalypse, fueled by an unbending faith in rock &amp; roll to beat back the darkness. Singer-guitarists Joe Strummer and Mick Jones wrote together in Jones&apos; grandmother&apos;s flat. &quot;Joe, once he learned how to type, would bang the lyrics out,&quot; Jones said. &quot;Then I&apos;d be able to bang out some music while he was hitting the typewriter.&quot; Strummer, Jones, bassist Paul Simonon and drummer Topper Headon spent nearly three months rehearsing and demoing songs in a garage in the Pimlico section of London &#x2013; &quot;with one light and filthy carpet on the walls for soundproofing,&quot; recalled Strummer in 1989. &quot;We felt that we were struggling,&quot; he said, &quot;about to slide down a slope or something, grasping with our fingernails.&quot; But Stevens was on hand for inspiration. {"id":351023,"title":"Marvin Gaye, 'What's Going On'","description":"","body":"<p><em>Motown, 1971</em></p><p>&quot;In 1969 or 1970, I began to re-evaluate my whole concept of what I wanted my music to say,&quot; Marvin Gaye said. &quot;I was very much affected by letters my brother was sending me from Vietnam, as well as the social situation here at home. As for &quot;Mercy Mercy Me (The Ecology),&quot; Gordy asserted that he didn&apos;t even know what the word &quot;ecology&quot; meant. Gaye responded that he would never record for Motown again unless &quot;What&apos;s Going On&quot; was released as a single. After initially being rejected by Motown&apos;s quality-control committee, it was; when the song became a Top Five hit, the album &#x2013; and a burst of socially conscious music from Motown &#x2013; followed soon after. {"id":351027,"title":"The Beatles, 'Revolver'","description":"","body":"<p><em>Capitol, 1966</em></p><p>&quot;I don&apos;t see too much difference between <em>Revolver</em> and <em>Rubber Soul</em>,&quot; George Harrison once said. &quot;To me, they could be Volume One and Volume Two.&quot; <em>Revolver</em> extends the more adventurous aspects of its predecessor &#x2013; its introspection, its nascent psychedelia, its fascination with studio artistry &#x2013; into a dramatic statement of generational possibility.</p><p>The album, which was released in August 1966, made it thrillingly clear that what we now think of as &quot;the Sixties&quot; was fully &#x2013; and irreversibly &#x2013; under way.</p><p>The most innovative track on the album is John Lennon&apos;s &quot;Tomorrow Never Knows.&quot; Attempting to distill an LSD trip into a three-minute song, Lennon borrowed lyrics from Timothy Leary&apos;s version of <em>The Tibetan Book of the Dead</em>, and recorded his vocal to sound like &quot;the Dalai Lama singing from the highest mountaintop.&quot; Tape loops, a backward guitar part (Paul McCartney&apos;s blistering solo on &quot;Taxman,&quot; in fact) and a droning tamboura completed the experimental effect, and the song proved hugely influential. For his part, on &quot;Eleanor Rigby&quot; and &quot;For No One,&quot; McCartney mastered a strikingly mature form of art song, and Harrison, with &quot;Taxman,&quot; &quot;I Want to Tell You&quot; and &quot;Love You To,&quot; challenged Lennon-McCartney&apos;s songwriting dominance.</p><p>Part of the album&apos;s revolutionary impulse was visual. Klaus Voormann, one of the Beatles&apos; artist buddies from their days in Hamburg, Germany, designed a striking photo-collage cover for <em>Revolver</em>; it was a crucial step on the road to the even trippier, more colorful imagery of <em>Sgt. Pepper&apos;s Lonely Hearts Club Band</em>, which would come less than a year later.</p><p><em>Revolver</em> signaled that in popular music, anything &#x2013; any theme, any musical idea &#x2013; could now be realized. And, in the case of the Beatles, would be.</p>","slug":"/music/lists/500-greatest-albums-of-all-time-20120531/the-beatles-revolver-20120524","order":499,"publish_date":"2012-05-24T15:50:13.000Z","updated_publish_date":null,"author":null,"media":{"lead":{"id":"","alt":"","code":"","type":"Variable Height Image","credits":"","details":"","filename":"","provider":""},"main_image":{"alt":null,"align":"full","caption":null,"credits":null,"filename":"rs-136792-daa031ec00ad83a2da305fe583d746d030fa46ca.jpg","leadType":"Variable Height Image"}},"bitly":"","embeds":{"related":{"content":{"pinned":[]}}},"sequence":3}
{"id":351028,"title":"The Beach Boys, 'Pet Sounds'","description":"","body":"<p><em>Capitol, 1966</em></p><p>&quot;Who&apos;s gonna hear this shit?&quot; Beach Boys singer Mike Love asked the band&apos;s resident genius, Brian Wilson, in 1966, as Wilson played him the new songs he was working on. &quot;The ears of a dog?&quot; But Love&apos;s contempt proved oddly useful: &quot;Ironically,&quot; Wilson observed, &quot;Mike&apos;s barb inspired the album&apos;s title.&quot; Barking dogs &#x2013; Wilson&apos;s dog Banana among them, in fact &#x2013; are prominent among the found sounds on the album. The Beatles made a point of echoing them on <em>Sgt. Pepper&apos;s Lonely Hearts Club Band</em> &#x2013; an acknowledgment that <em>Pet Sounds</em> was the inspiration for the Beatles&apos; masterpiece. That gesture actually completed a circle of influence: Wilson initially conceived of <em>Pet Sounds</em> as an effort to top the Beatles&apos; <em>Rubber Soul</em>.</p><p>With its vivid orchestration, lyrical ambition, elegant pacing and thematic coherence, <em>Pet Sounds</em> invented &#x2013; and in some sense perfected &#x2013; the idea that an album could be more than the sum of its parts. When Wilson sang, &quot;Wouldn&apos;t it be nice if we were older?&quot; on the magnificent opener, he wasn&apos;t just imagining a love that could evolve past high school; he was suggesting a new grown-up identity for rock &amp; roll music itself.</p><p>Wilson essentially made <em>Pet Sounds</em> without the rest of the band, using them only to flesh out the vocal arrangements. (He even considered putting the album out as a solo project, and the first single, &quot;Caroline, No,&quot; was released under his own name.) Its luxurious sound conveys a heartbreaking wistfulness, and the deeply personal songs, which Wilson co-wrote primarily with lyricist Tony Asher, bid farewell to the innocent world of the Beach Boys&apos; fun-in-the-sun hits. Unfortunately, Capitol Records proved no more enamored of <em>Pet Sounds</em> than had Love; the label considered not releasing it at all. Not yet vindicated by history, Wilson withdrew further into his inner world. &quot;At the last meeting I attended concerning <em>Pet Sounds</em>,&quot; Wilson wrote about his dealings with the label, &quot;I showed up holding a tape player and eight prerecorded, looped responses, including &apos;No comment,&apos; &apos;Can you repeat that?&apos; &apos;No&apos; and &apos;Yes.&apos; Refusing to utter a word, I played the various tapes when appropriate.&quot;</p>","slug":"/music/lists/500-greatest-albums-of-all-time-20120531/the-beach-boys-pet-sounds-20120524","order":500,"publish_date":"2012-05-24T15:41:24.000Z","updated_publish_date":null,"author":null,"media":{"lead":{"id":"","alt":"","code":"","type":"Variable Height Image","credits":"","details":"","filename":"","provider":""},"main_image":{"alt":null,"align":"full","caption":null,"credits":null,"filename":"rs-136791-eafbc592120358b4d3d14cda5acebf62ae50a522.jpg","leadType":"Variable Height Image"}},"bitly":"","embeds":{"related":{"content":{"pinned":[]}}},"sequence":2}
{"id":351029,"title":"The Beatles, 'Sgt. Pepper's Lonely Hearts Club Band'","description":"","body":"<p><em>Capitol, 1967</em></p><p><em>Sgt. Pepper&apos;s Lonely Hearts Club Band</em> is the most important rock &amp; roll album ever made, an unsurpassed adventure in concept, sound, songwriting, cover art and studio technology by the greatest rock &amp; roll group of all time. From the title song&apos;s regal blasts of brass and fuzz guitar to the orchestral seizure and long, dying piano chord at the end of &quot;A Day in the Life,&quot; the 13 tracks on <em>Sgt. Pepper&apos;s Lonely Hearts Club Band</em> are the pinnacle of the Beatles&apos; eight years as recording artists. John Lennon, Paul McCartney, George Harrison and Ringo Starr were never more fearless and unified in their pursuit of magic and transcendence.</p><p>Issued in Britain on June 1st, 1967, and a day later in America, <em>Sgt. Pepper</em> is also rock&apos;s ultimate declaration of change. For the Beatles, it was a decisive goodbye to matching suits, world tours and assembly-line record-making. &quot;We were fed up with being Beatles,&quot; McCartney said decades later, in <em>Many Years From Now</em>, Barry Miles&apos; McCartney biography. &quot;We were not boys, we were men... artists rather than performers.</p><p>At the same time, <em>Sgt. Pepper</em> formally ushered in an unforgettable season of hope, upheaval and achievement: the late 1960s and, in particular, 1967&apos;s Summer of Love. In its iridescent instrumentation, lyric fantasias and eye-popping packaging, <em>Sgt. Pepper</em> defined the opulent revolutionary optimism of psychedelia and instantly spread the gospel of love, acid, Eastern spirituality and electric guitars around the globe. No other pop record of that era, or since, has had such an immediate, titanic impact. This music documents the world&apos;s biggest rock band at the very height of its influence and ambition.</p><p>&quot;It was a peak,&quot; Lennon told <em>Rolling Stone</em> in 1970, describing both the album and his collaborative relationship with McCartney. &quot;Paul and I were definitely working together,&quot; Lennon said, and Sgt. Pepper is rich with proof: McCartney&apos;s burst of hot piano and school-days memoir (&quot;Woke up, fell out of bed...&quot;) in Lennon&apos;s &quot;A Day in the Life,&quot; a reverie on mortality and infinity; Lennon&apos;s impish rejoinder to McCartney&apos;s chorus in &quot;Getting Better&quot; (&quot;It can&apos;t get no worse&quot;).</p><p>&quot;<em>Sgt. Pepper</em> was our grandest endeavor,&quot; Starr said, looking back, in the band&apos;s 2000 autobiography, <em>The Beatles Anthology. </em>&quot;The greatest thing about the band was that whoever had the best idea &#x2013; it didn&apos;t matter who &#x2013; that was the one we&apos;d use.&quot; It was Neil Aspinall, the Beatles&apos; longtime assistant, who suggested they reprise the title track, just before the finale of &quot;A Day in the Life,&quot; to complete <em>Sgt. Pepper</em>&apos;s theatrical conceit: an imaginary concert by a fictional band, played by the Beatles.</p><p>The first notes went to tape on December 6th, 1966: two takes of McCartney&apos;s music-hall confection &quot;When I&apos;m Sixty-Four.&quot; (Lennon&apos;s lysergic reflection on his Liverpool childhood, &quot;Strawberry Fields Forever,&quot; was started two weeks earlier but issued in February 1967 as a stand-alone single.) But <em>Sgt. Pepper</em>&apos;s real birthday is August 29th, 1966, when the Beatles played their last live concert, in San Francisco. Until then, they had made history in the studio between punishing tours. Off the road for good, the Beatles were free to be a band away from the hysteria of Beatlemania.</p><p>McCartney went a step further. On a plane to London in November &apos;66, as he returned from a vacation in Kenya, he came up with the idea of an album by the Beatles in disguise, an alter-ego group that he subsequently dubbed Sgt. Pepper&apos;s Lonely Hearts Club Band. &quot;We&apos;d pretend to be someone else,&quot; McCartney explained in <em>Anthology</em>. &quot;It liberated you &#x2013; you could do anything when you got to the mic or on your guitar, because it wasn&apos;t you.&quot;</p><p>Only two songs on the final LP, both McCartney&apos;s, had anything to do with the Pepper characters: the title track and Starr&apos;s jaunty vocal showcase, &quot;With a Little Help From My Friends,&quot; introduced as a number by <em>Sgt. Pepper</em>&apos;s star crooner, Billy Shears. &quot;Every other song could have been on any other album,&quot; Lennon insisted later. Yet it is hard to imagine a more perfect setting for the Victorian jollity of Lennon&apos;s &quot;Being for the Benefit of Mr. Kite!&quot; (inspired by an 1843 circus poster) or the sumptuous melancholy of McCartney&apos;s &quot;Fixing a Hole,&quot; with its blend of antique shadows (a harpsichord played by the Beatles&apos; producer, George Martin) and modern sunshine (double-tracked lead guitar executed with ringing precision by Harrison). The <em>Pepper</em> premise was a license to thrill.</p><p>It also underscored the real-life cohesion of the music and the group that made it. Of the 700 hours the Beatles spent making <em>Sgt. Pepper</em> from the end of 1966 until April 1967, the group needed only three days&apos; worth to complete Lennon&apos;s lavish daydream &quot;Lucy in the Sky With Diamonds.&quot; &quot;A Day in the Life,&quot; the most complex song on the album, was done in just five days. (The oceanic piano chord was three pianos hit simultaneously by 10 hands belonging to Lennon, McCartney, Starr, Martin and Beatles roadie Mal Evans.) No other Beatles appear with Harrison on his sitar-perfumed sermon on materialism and fidelity, &quot;Within You Without You,&quot; but the band wisely placed the track at the halfway point of the original vinyl LP, at the beginning of Side Two: a vital meditation break in the middle of the jubilant indulgence.</p><p>The Beatles&apos; exploitation of multitracking transformed the very act of studio recording (the orchestral overdubs on &quot;A Day in the Life&quot; marked the debut of eight-track recording in Britain: two four-track machines used in sync). And <em>Sgt. Pepper</em>&apos;s visual extravagance officially elevated the album cover to a work of art. Michael Cooper&apos;s photo of the Beat&#xAD;les in satin marching-band outfits, in front of a cardboard-cutout audience of historical figures, created by artist Peter Blake, is the most enduring image of the psychedelic era. <em>Sgt. Pepper</em> was also the first rock album to incorporate complete lyrics to the songs in its design.</p><p>Yet <em>Sgt. Pepper</em> is the Number One album of the RS 500 not just because of its firsts &#x2013; it is simply the best of everything the Beatles ever did as musicians, pioneers and pop stars, all in one place. A 1967 British print ad for the album declared, &quot;Remember, Sgt. Pepper&apos;s Lonely Hearts Club Band Is the Beatles.&quot; As McCartney put it, the album was &quot;just us doing a good show.&quot;</p><p>The show goes on forever.</p><p><strong>Voters:&#xA0;</strong>Bill Adler (Biographer, Run-DMC), Lou Adler (Producer), Vince Aletti (Writer), Art Alexakis (Everclear), Pelle Almqvist (The Hives), Billy Altman (Writer), Jeff Ament (Pearl Jam), Roger Ames (Former chairman and CEO,&#xA0;Warner Music Group), Billie Joe Armstrong (Green Day), Nicholaus Arson (The Hives), Dick Asher (Former CEO, Polygram Records), James Austin (Former A&amp;R, Rhino Records), Michael Azerrad (Writer), Irving Azoff (Executive chairman, Live Nation), Martin Bandier (Chairman and CEO,&#xA0;Sony/ATV), Devendra Banhart, Peter Barakan (Radio host), Johnny Barbis (Chairman, Rocket Music), Ken Barnes (Writer), Kevin Barnes&#xA0;(Of Montreal), Frank Barsalona (Former consultant, William Morris Agency), Rostam Batmanglij (Vampire Weekend), David Bauder (Writer), Beck, Jules Belkin (Former president, Belkin Productions),Andy Bell (Erasure), Bill Belmont (Former VP, international operations, Fantasy Records), Bill Bentley (Director, A&amp;R, Vanguard Records), Steve Berkowitz (Senior VP, A&amp;R,&#xA0; Legacy Recordings), James Bernard (Co-founder, <em>The Source</em>&#xA0;and <em>XXL</em> magazines), Cathy Bernardy Jones (Former editor, <em>Goldmine</em> magazine), Guy Berryman (Coldplay), Jim Bessman (Writer), Les Bider (Former chairman and CEO, Warner/Chappell Music), Scott Billington (VP, A&amp;R, Rounder Records), Mark Binelli (Contributing editor,&#xA0;<em>Rolling Stone),&#xA0;</em>Rodney Bingenheimer (Radio personality), David Bither (Senior VP, Nonesuch Records), Hal Blaine (Drummer), Jerry Blavat (Radio and TV personality), Mary J.
